Scenarios on Wala' al-Muwalah with Children, Captives, and Non-Muslims
Casual enumeration of rulings on muwalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on wala' al-muwalah to scenarios involving children, captives, dhimmis, harbis, and acknowledgment of wala'.

Core Idea
Muwalah is a valid contract with specific rules on drawing, suspension, and inheritance.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of detailed scenarios illustrating how wala' al-muwalah operates in complex family and interfaith situations. Understanding these cases clarifies the boundaries of the pact-based clientage system and highlights key intra-Hanafi differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their relationships
For each scenario, note who is involved (father, son, captive, dhimmi, etc.) and their existing status (Muslim, harbi, freed, etc.).
The wala' relationship can be based on emancipation ('itq) or pact (muwalah).
2
Determine the effect on wala'
Apply the rules: an adult child is independent; a minor child follows the father's patron; captivity and freeing can draw wala' from one to another.
Drawing (jarr) transfers wala' from a relative to the freed person.
3
Note the conditions of the pact
Muwalah cannot be suspended on future events; it is a binding contract. Religion is not a barrier for the pact but blocks inheritance.
The pact is valid between a dhimmi and a Muslim, but inheritance requires religious unity.
4
Identify intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa differs from the Sahibayn on the child of a dhimmi mother and on the validity of acknowledgment of wala' after contradictory claims.
The majority view follows the Sahibayn in these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قولهما، وفي قياس قول الإمام يكون له أسلم رجل على أن يكون ولاؤه لأول ولد له لا يجوز لأن عقد الموالاة لا يجوز تعليقه بالإخطار، فلو قال إن واليتك إن فعلت كذا لم يصح، وإن كان لمن عقد عقد الموالاة ولد كبير فإذا أسلم ابنه الكبير على يد رجل ووالاه فولاؤه له لأنه أولى بنفسه لانقطاع ولاية الأب، وإن أسلم ولم يوال أحداً فولاؤه موقوف بخلاف ولاء العتاقة فإن الولد الكبير يتبع الأب في ولاء العتاقة لأن الكبير يستنصر من يوالي أبيه. رجل والى رجلاً ثم ولد له من امرأته ولد فوالت رجلاً فولاء الولد لمولى الأب، وإذا والى رجلاً وابنه الكبير رجلاً كان كل واحد لمولاه ولا يجر بعضهم بعضاً، فإن ابنه سبي وأعتق لم يجر ولاء أبيه، فإن سبي أبوه وأعتق جر ولاء الابن لأن الابن بنسب إلى الأب فكذا في الموالاة. فإن كان له ابن ابن والابن لم يسب لكن أسلم فوالاه رجل فسبي الجد فأعتق لم يجر الجد ولاءه إلا أن يجر ولاء ابنه فيتجر حتى لو كان الأسفل موالياً حربياً والجد معتق لا يجر إلا أن يسلم الأوسط فيجره الجد فينجر بجره. أسلم الحربي ولم يوال أحداً ثم أعتق أبوه جر ولاءه، ولو أسلم أبوه ووالى رجلاً لم يجر. والي ذمي مسلماً أو ذمياً جاز وهو مولاه لأنه يجوز أن يكون للذمي على المسلم ولاء العتاقة فكذا ولاء الموالاة فإن قلت : قال في المحيط ذمي والى مسلماً فمات لم يرثه لأن الإرث باعتبار التناصر والتناصر في غير القرب إنما هو بالدين، فهذا يفيد أن الموالاة لا تكون بين المسلم والذمي قلنا هي تكون بينهما لكن الإرث إنما يكون حيث لا مانع وحينئذ المانع هنا وهو اختلاف الدين. وإن أسلم على يد ووالاء لم يذكره في الكتاب واختلفوا فيما إذا أعتق الحربي عبده المسلم؛ قيل يصح حربي لأنه لأنه يجوز أن يكون للحربي على المسلم ولاء العتاقة فكذا ولاء الموالاة، وقيل لا يصح عقد المولاة مع الحربي للتناصر وقد نهينا عن ذلك بخلاف الذمي .اهـ. وفي المبسوط : رجل اشترى من رجل عبداً ثم شهد أن البائع كان أعتقه فهو حر وولاؤه موقوف إذا جحد البائع ذلك فإن صدقه البائع بعد ذلك ظهر أنه المولى وكذا إن صدقه الورثة بعد موته. وفي التتارخانية : رجل من أهل الذمة أعتق عبداً فنقض الذمي العهد ولحق بدار الحرب فأخذ واسترق فصار عبداً لرجل وأراد معتقه أن يوالي رجلاً لم يكن له ذلك لأن مولى العتاقة لا يملك أن يوالي أحداً، فإن أعتق مولاه يوماً من الدهر فإنه يرثه، وإن جنى جناية عقل عن نفسه ولا يعقل عنه مولاه ؛ هكذا ذكر في عامة الروايات وفي بعضها قال : يرثه ويعقل عنه . وإذا أقر الرجل بالولاء لآخر وصدقه يصير مولى له يعقل عنه ويرثه، فإن كان له أولاد كبار فكذبوا الأب فيما أقر وقالوا أبونا مولى لفلان آخر وصدقهم فلان في ذلك فهم مصدقون في حق أنفسهم، وإن قال أعتقني فلان أو فلان وكل منهما يدعي أنه المعتق لا يلزم العبد شيء، وإن أقر بعد ذلك لأحدهما بعينه أو لغيرهما يجوز إقراره على قولهما، وعلى قول الإمام لا يجوز إذا أقر الرجل أنه مولى امرأة أعتقته فقالت المرأة لم أعتقك ليكن أسلمت على
According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to the analogy of the statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa), it would belong to him. A man became Muslim on the condition that his wala' be for his first child; this is not permissible because the muwalah contract cannot be suspended on contingencies. So if he said, 'I make a pact with you if you do such-and-such,' it is not valid. If the person who made the muwalah contract has an adult son, and his adult son becomes Muslim at the hands of a man and makes a pact, then his wala' belongs to that man because the adult son is independent of his father's authority. If he becomes Muslim and does not make a pact with anyone, his wala' is suspended, unlike wala' of emancipation where an adult child follows the father because the adult seeks support from those who have a pact with his father. A man made a pact with a man, then a son was born to him from his wife, and [the son] made a pact with a man; the wala' of the son belongs to the father's patron. If a man makes a pact with a man, and his adult son makes a pact with another man, each belongs to his own patron, and one does not draw the other. If his son is captured and freed, he does not draw the wala' of the father. If his father is captured and freed, he draws the wala' of the son because the son is attributed to the father, and similarly in muwalah. If there is a grandson and the son has not been captured but became Muslim and made a pact with a man, then the grandfather is captured and freed; the grandfather does not draw his wala' unless he draws the wala' of his son, then it becomes drawn. So if the inferior is in a muwalah with a harbi and the grandfather is a freedman, he does not draw unless the middle one becomes Muslim, then the grandfather draws him and becomes drawn by his drawing. A harbi became Muslim and did not make a pact with anyone, then his father freed him, drawing his wala'. If his father became Muslim and made a pact with a man, he does not draw. A dhimmi made a pact with a Muslim or a dhimmi, it is valid, and he is his patron, because a dhimmi can have wala' of emancipation over a Muslim, and similarly wala' of muwalah. If it is said: In al-Muhit, a dhimmi made a pact with a Muslim and died, the Muslim does not inherit from him because inheritance is based on mutual support, and mutual support for non-relatives is only through religion. This indicates that muwalah cannot be between a Muslim and a dhimmi. We say: it can occur between them, but inheritance only occurs where there is no impediment, and here the impediment is difference of religion. And if he became Muslim at his hands and made a pact, it is not mentioned in the book. And they differed concerning when a harbi frees his Muslim slave: some say it is valid for a harbi because a harbi can have wala' of emancipation over a Muslim, and similarly wala' of muwalah. Some say the muwalah contract is not valid with a harbi due to mutual support, and we have been forbidden from that, unlike a dhimmi. End quote. In al-Mabsut: A man bought a slave from a man, then testified that the seller had freed him; the slave is free and his wala' is suspended if the seller denies it. If the seller later confirms it, it becomes clear that he is the patron, and likewise if the heirs confirm after his death. In al-Tatar Khaniyah: A man from the dhimmis freed a slave, then the dhimmi broke the covenant and joined the dar al-harb, was captured and enslaved, becoming a slave of a man. His former master wanted to make a muwalah pact with another man; he does not have that right because the patron of emancipation does not have the right to make a pact with anyone. If his master frees him someday, he inherits from him; if the slave commits a crime, he pays the blood money from himself, and his master does not pay for him. This is mentioned in most narrations, and in some it is said: he inherits from him and pays for him. If a man acknowledges wala' to another and the other confirms it, he becomes his patron, paying blood money and inheriting from him. If he has adult children who deny what their father acknowledged and say: 'Our father is the patron of so-and-so,' and that so-and-so confirms them, then they are believed regarding themselves. If a man says: 'So-and-so freed me' or 'So-and-so freed me,' and each claims to be the one who freed him, the slave incurs no obligation. If he later acknowledges one of them specifically or someone else, his acknowledgment is valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is not valid. If a man acknowledges that he is the patron of a woman who freed him, and the woman says, 'I did not free you; rather, you became Muslim at my hands...'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents numerous scenarios related to wala' al-muwalah, continuing from the previous discussion. It begins with the intra-Hanafi difference on the child of a dhimmi woman who converts and makes a muwalah pact: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, her child's wala' does not belong to her patron, but by Abu Hanifa's analogy it would. It then discusses the invalidity of suspending muwalah on future events, the independence of adult children, the effect of captivity and emancipation on wala', and the possibility of muwalah between a dhimmi and a Muslim (valid, but inheritance is blocked by difference of religion). It covers cases of acknowledgment of wala', denial by heirs, and contradictory claims about emancipation. The passage ends with an incomplete scenario about a woman who claims a man became Muslim at her hands rather than being freed. Throughout, the Hanafi school's detailed casuistry is displayed, showing how wala' is transferred, suspended, or blocked in various family and captivity situations.

Key Terms
الجر (al-jarr): The legal pulling or drawing of wala' from a relative to oneself, such as when a freed father draws the wala' of his son.
الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of mutual clientage between two free persons, establishing a patron-client relationship with inheritance and blood money obligations.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending a contract on a future event, which is invalid in muwalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a dhimmi makes a muwalah pact with a Muslim, and the dhimmi dies, does the Muslim inherit from him?
A) Yes, because the pact is valid
B) No, because of difference of religion
C) Yes, if the dhimmi had no other heirs
D) No, because muwalah is not valid with a dhimmi

Key Takeaway
Muwalah is a nuanced contract with specific rules on contingency, drawing, and the impact of captivity, religion, and family relationships.

Invalidity of Conditional Muwalah
Ruling on invalid conditional wala'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a muwalah contract is invalid if conditioned on a future contingency and explain the reason based on the prohibition of ta'liq bi al-ikhtiyar.

Core Idea
Muwalah must be immediate and unconditional; any condition on a future event invalidates it.

Many assume that contracts can be made conditional. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that muwalah—a contract of clientage—cannot be suspended on an uncertain event. We will examine the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a man accepting Islam with the condition that his wala' be for his firstborn. The ruling is that it is not permissible (batil).
The condition makes the entire muwalah contract invalid.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the general principle that muwalah cannot be conditioned on a contingency (ta'liq bi al-ikhtiyar).
This is derived from the nature of muwalah as a gratuitous contract.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Muwalah requires immediate effect; conditioning it on a future event introduces uncertainty (gharar) and delays the transfer of wala', which contradicts the contract's purpose.
The Hanafi school treats gratuitous contracts strictly to avoid exploitation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Both disciples and the analogy of Abu Hanifa's view agree; no intra-Hanafi dispute on this point.
The ruling is from zahir al-riwayah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The ruling is relied upon without need for tarjih due to unanimity among the three imams.
The mufta bihi is that the conditional contract is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قولهما، وفي قياس قول الإمام يكون له أسلم رجل على أن يكون ولاؤه لأول ولد له لا يجوز لأن عقد الموالاة لا يجوز تعليقه بالإخطار، فلو قال إن واليتك إن فعلت كذا لم يصح، وإن كان لمن عقد عقد الموالاة ولد كبير فإذا أسلم ابنه الكبير على يد رجل ووالاه فولاؤه له لأنه أولى بنفسه لانقطاع ولاية الأب، وإن أسلم ولم يوال أحداً فولاؤه موقوف بخلاف ولاء العتاقة فإن الولد الكبير يتبع الأب في ولاء العتاقة لأن الكبير يستنصر من يوالي أبيه.
According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to the analogy of the Imam's statement: if a man accepts Islam on the condition that his wala' is for his firstborn, it is not permissible because the contract of muwalah cannot be conditioned on a contingency. So if he said, 'I will enter into muwalah with you if you do such-and-such,' it is not valid. And if the one who makes a contract of muwalah has an adult son, and his adult son becomes Muslim at the hand of a man and enters into muwalah with him, then his wala' belongs to that man because he is primary over himself due to the termination of the father's guardianship. And if he becomes Muslim and does not enter into muwalah with anyone, his wala' is suspended. This is different from wala' of emancipation, for the adult son follows the father in the wala' of emancipation because the adult seeks support from the patron of his father.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a ruling regarding the contract of muwalah (clientage by agreement). The text begins by stating that both the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the analogy of Imam Abu Hanifa's view agree: if a person accepts Islam and stipulates that his wala' will go to his firstborn child, such a condition is invalid. The reason is that a muwalah contract cannot be suspended on an uncertain future event (ta'liq bi al-ikhtiyar). The author illustrates with an example: if one says, 'I will enter muwalah with you if you do such-and-such,' the contract is not valid because it is conditional. Then the text contrasts the case of an adult son: if the son embraces Islam at someone's hand and enters muwalah, his wala' belongs to that patron, not his father, because the adult son is independent of his father's authority. If he becomes Muslim but does not enter muwalah, his wala' remains suspended (i.e., he is not attached to any patron). This differs from wala' al-itiq (emancipation), where an adult son follows his father's wala' because he seeks support from his father's patron. The key principle is that muwalah is a voluntary contract, not a mandatory chain like emancipation.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of clientage between two free persons, whereby one becomes a patron and the other a client, with mutual obligations of support and inheritance.
التعليق بالإخطار (al-ta'liq bi al-ikhtiyar): Conditioning a contract on a future contingent event that is within the power of one party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a muwalah contract conditioned on a future event is:
A) Valid if the event is likely to occur.
B) Invalid because muwalah cannot be suspended on a contingency.
C) Valid only if the condition is within the power of the party imposing it.
D) Invalid unless the condition is for the benefit of the client.

Key Takeaway
Muwalah is a gratuitous contract that must be unconditional; any condition on a future event renders it invalid.

Adult Son's Independent Wala' in Muwalah vs Following Father in Emancipation
Ruling on adult son's independent wala' in muwalah vs following in emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the adult son's independent wala' in muwalah from his following the father in wala' al-itiq and identify the reason for the difference.

Core Idea
An adult son is independent in muwalah but follows his father in emancipation because muwalah is voluntary while emancipation's wala' is a necessity.

The previous lesson dealt with conditional muwalah. Now we turn to the adult son's position: does he follow his father's wala' or is he independent? The Hanafi school differentiates between muwalah and emancipation. This passage explains the rule and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for muwalah
If an adult son enters muwalah with someone independently, his wala' belongs to that patron, not his father's patron.
The adult son is primary over himself (huwa awla bi nafsih).
2
State the rule if no muwalah
If he becomes Muslim but does not contract muwalah with anyone, his wala' is suspended (mawquf).
He has no patron until he chooses one.
3
Contrast with emancipation
In wala' al-itiq, the adult son follows the father's wala' even if he does not choose a patron himself.
The reason: the adult son seeks support from his father's patron (yastansir min muwali abih).
4
Identify the illa (reason)
Muwalah is a contract of free choice; the adult son is independent of paternal authority. Emancipation's wala' is a legal consequence of freeing, and the son's connection is through necessity (nasr).
The father's authority over an adult son is broken (inqita' wilayat al-ab).
5
Evaluate the ruling
The ruling is clear and from the text; no dispute mentioned.
It is a straightforward application of the principle of individual autonomy.

Source Text

الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of clientage between two free persons, whereby one becomes a patron and the other a client, with mutual obligations of support and inheritance.
ولاء العتاقة (wala' al-itiq): The clientage that arises when a master frees a slave; the freed slave's wala' belongs to the master (patron) and passes to his heirs.
انقطاع ولاية الأب (inqita' wilayat al-ab): Termination of the father's guardianship over an adult son, meaning the son is legally independent.

Child's Wala' Follows Father's Patron; Father and Adult Son Have Independent Wala'
Rulings on child's wala' following father and non-transfer between father and son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a child's wala' follows the father's patron and explain that a father and adult son do not transfer wala' between each other's patrons.

Core Idea
A child's wala' follows the father's patron; independent muwalah between father and adult son keeps wala' separate.

This lesson focuses on two foundational rules from the passage: first, when a man enters muwalah and later has a child, the child's wala' automatically belongs to the father's patron. Second, if a father and his adult son each independently enter muwalah with different patrons, their wala' remain distinct—neither brings the other's wala'. Understanding these rules is essential for determining how patronage rights spread through family relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario: child born after father's muwalah
The passage begins with a man who entered muwalah, then later a child is born to him. Note that the mother also entered muwalah with a different man.
The child is born after the father's muwalah, not before.
2
State the ruling for the child's wala'
The text says: 'the wala' of the child belongs to the patron of the father.' This is because the child follows the father's lineage and contract.
Even if the mother has a separate muwalah, her patron does not get the child's wala'.
3
Identify the second scenario: father and adult son each enter muwalah
A father enters muwalah with one man, and his adult son enters muwalah with another man. The son is independent, not a minor.
The son is 'kabir' (adult), so he has his own capacity.
4
State the ruling for father and adult son
Each one belongs to his own patron, and they do not bring each other's wala' to their respective patrons. There is no jarr between them.
This shows that adult relatives do not automatically transfer wala'.
5
Understand the principle: no jarr between father and adult son
Jarr (bringing wala') operates only in specific circumstances, such as when a father is captured and freed, as we will see in the next lesson. Here, independent contracts remain separate.
The exception is when one relative is captured and emancipated—that triggers jarr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل والى رجلاً ثم ولد له من امرأته ولد فوالت رجلاً فولاء الولد لمولى الأب، وإذا والى رجلاً وابنه الكبير رجلاً كان كل واحد لمولاه ولا يجر بعضهم بعضاً، فإن ابنه سبي وأعتق لم يجر ولاء أبيه، فإن سبي أبوه وأعتق جر ولاء الابن لأن الابن بنسب إلى الأب فكذا في الموالاة. فإن كان له ابن ابن والابن لم يسب لكن أسلم فوالاه رجل فسبي الجد فأعتق لم يجر الجد ولاءه إلا أن يجر ولاء ابنه فيتجر حتى لو كان الأسفل موالياً حربياً والجد معتق لا يجر إلا أن يسلم الأوسط فيجره الجد فينجر بجره. أسلم الحربي ولم يوال أحداً ثم أعتق أبوه جر ولاءه، ولو أسلم أبوه ووالى رجلاً لم يجر.
A man entered into muwalah with a man, then a child was born to him from his wife, and she entered into muwalah with a man. The wala' of the child belongs to the patron of the father. And if a man entered into muwalah with a man, and his adult son entered into muwalah with a man, each one belongs to his own patron, and they do not bring each other (the wala') over. Then if his son is captured and emancipated, he does not bring the wala' of his father. If his father is captured and emancipated, he brings the wala' of the son, because the son is attributed to the father, and likewise in muwalah. And if he has a grandson, and the son is not captured but becomes Muslim and enters into muwalah with a man, then the grandfather is captured and emancipated; the grandfather does not bring his wala' unless he brings the wala' of his son, and then it is brought; even if the lower one is in muwalah with a war captive and the grandfather is emancipated, he does not bring unless the middle one becomes Muslim, then the grandfather brings him and it is brought by his bringing. The war captive became Muslim and did not enter into muwalah with anyone, then his father emancipated him; he brings his wala'. And if his father became Muslim and entered into muwalah with a man, he does not bring (his son's wala').
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage gives specific rulings on how wala' operates within families when muwalah is involved. First, if a man has a child after he himself has entered muwalah, that child's wala' belongs to the father's patron, even if the mother separately entered muwalah with someone else; the child follows the father's patron. Second, if a father and his adult son each independently enter muwalah with different persons, their wala' are separate; neither brings the other's wala' to his own patron. Third, the text discusses captivity and emancipation: if a son is captured and then freed by someone, that does not bring his father's wala' to his new patron. But if the father is captured and freed, he does bring the son's wala' because the son is connected to the father through lineage. Fourth, the case of a grandfather and grandson: if the son does not enter muwalah but the grandson does, and then the grandfather is freed, the grandfather cannot bring the grandson's wala' unless he brings the intermediate son's wala' (i.e., if the son is already a client). Fifth, a war captive who becomes Muslim without entering muwalah will have his wala' brought by his father if the father later emancipates him. But if the father becomes Muslim and enters muwalah with someone, he does not bring his son's wala'. The underlying principle is that wala' follows lineage and prior contracts, but it is not automatically transferred between relatives unless there is a specific connection.

Key Terms
الجر (al-jarr): The legal effect of one person's wala' bringing another's wala' to the same patron, based on lineage or contract.
الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of clientage (wala') between two free persons, where one pledges loyalty to the other, establishing inheritance rights and patronage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a father and his adult son each enter muwalah with different persons, what is the status of their wala'?
A) The son's wala' transfers to the father's patron.
B) The father's wala' transfers to the son's patron.
C) Each belongs to his own patron; no transfer occurs.
D) The wala' is suspended until a captivity event.

Key Takeaway
A child born after the father's muwalah follows the father's patron, but an adult son's independent muwalah remains separate; no automatic jarr between father and adult son.

Jarr: Bringing Wala' Between Relatives in Captivity and Emancipation
Rulings on child's wala' following father and non-transfer between father and son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of jarr (bringing wala') in scenarios of captivity, emancipation, and lineage between father, son, and grandson.

Core Idea
Wala' is brought from son to father by captivity but not from father to son; lineage determines the direction of jarr.

This lesson delves into the intricate cases of jarr (bringing wala') when relatives are captured and emancipated. The passage presents a series of rulings that reveal a consistent principle: jarr follows the natural lineage relationship, where the wala' of a lower relative (e.g., son) can be brought to a higher relative's patron when the higher relative is captured and freed, but not vice versa. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly attributing wala' in complex family dynamics.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the son-captured scenario
If a son is captured and later emancipated (by someone else), does this bring the father's wala'? The text says no: 'his son is captured and emancipated, he does not bring the wala' of his father.'
The son's new wala' remains separate; the father's previous wala' is not affected.
2
Analyze the father-captured scenario
If the father is captured and emancipated, he does bring the wala' of the son. The text states: 'the son is attributed to the father, and likewise in muwalah.'
The direction is from father to son, because the son's lineage flows from the father.
3
Examine the grandfather-grandson case
If a grandson (son's son) enters muwalah, and the grandfather is later captured and freed, the grandfather does not directly bring the grandson's wala' unless he first brings the intermediate son's wala'.
The intermediate son must be in a position to be brought; if the son is not captured or has no wala', the grandfather cannot reach the grandson.
4
Understand the war captive scenario
A war captive (harbi) becomes Muslim without entering muwalah. If his father later emancipates him, the father brings the son's wala' because the son is freed by the father's action.
The father's emancipation creates a new wala' relationship that includes the son.
5
Contrast with the father-enters-muwalah scenario
If the father himself becomes Muslim and enters muwalah with a patron, he does not bring his son's wala' (who might have been freed elsewhere). This is because the father's muwalah is independent of the son's status.
Jarr only occurs when the active relative (the one captured/freed) is the higher in lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل والى رجلاً ثم ولد له من امرأته ولد فوالت رجلاً فولاء الولد لمولى الأب، وإذا والى رجلاً وابنه الكبير رجلاً كان كل واحد لمولاه ولا يجر بعضهم بعضاً، فإن ابنه سبي وأعتق لم يجر ولاء أبيه، فإن سبي أبوه وأعتق جر ولاء الابن لأن الابن بنسب إلى الأب فكذا في الموالاة. فإن كان له ابن ابن والابن لم يسب لكن أسلم فوالاه رجل فسبي الجد فأعتق لم يجر الجد ولاءه إلا أن يجر ولاء ابنه فيتجر حتى لو كان الأسفل موالياً حربياً والجد معتق لا يجر إلا أن يسلم الأوسط فيجره الجد فينجر بجره. أسلم الحربي ولم يوال أحداً ثم أعتق أبوه جر ولاءه، ولو أسلم أبوه ووالى رجلاً لم يجر.
A man entered into muwalah with a man, then a child was born to him from his wife, and she entered into muwalah with a man. The wala' of the child belongs to the patron of the father. And if a man entered into muwalah with a man, and his adult son entered into muwalah with a man, each one belongs to his own patron, and they do not bring each other (the wala') over. Then if his son is captured and emancipated, he does not bring the wala' of his father. If his father is captured and emancipated, he brings the wala' of the son, because the son is attributed to the father, and likewise in muwalah. And if he has a grandson, and the son is not captured but becomes Muslim and enters into muwalah with a man, then the grandfather is captured and emancipated; the grandfather does not bring his wala' unless he brings the wala' of his son, and then it is brought; even if the lower one is in muwalah with a war captive and the grandfather is emancipated, he does not bring unless the middle one becomes Muslim, then the grandfather brings him and it is brought by his bringing. The war captive became Muslim and did not enter into muwalah with anyone, then his father emancipated him; he brings his wala'. And if his father became Muslim and entered into muwalah with a man, he does not bring (his son's wala').
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage gives specific rulings on how wala' operates within families when muwalah is involved. First, if a man has a child after he himself has entered muwalah, that child's wala' belongs to the father's patron, even if the mother separately entered muwalah with someone else; the child follows the father's patron. Second, if a father and his adult son each independently enter muwalah with different persons, their wala' are separate; neither brings the other's wala' to his own patron. Third, the text discusses captivity and emancipation: if a son is captured and then freed by someone, that does not bring his father's wala' to his new patron. But if the father is captured and freed, he does bring the son's wala' because the son is connected to the father through lineage. Fourth, the case of a grandfather and grandson: if the son does not enter muwalah but the grandson does, and then the grandfather is freed, the grandfather cannot bring the grandson's wala' unless he brings the intermediate son's wala' (i.e., if the son is already a client). Fifth, a war captive who becomes Muslim without entering muwalah will have his wala' brought by his father if the father later emancipates him. But if the father becomes Muslim and enters muwalah with someone, he does not bring his son's wala'. The underlying principle is that wala' follows lineage and prior contracts, but it is not automatically transferred between relatives unless there is a specific connection.

Key Terms
الجر (al-jarr): The legal effect of one person's wala' bringing another's wala' to the same patron, based on lineage or contract.
الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of clientage (wala') between two free persons, where one pledges loyalty to the other, establishing inheritance rights and patronage.
السبي (al-saby): Captivity in war; a state where a free person is taken as a captive and later may be emancipated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario does jarr (bringing wala') occur?
A) A son is captured and emancipated; his father's wala' is brought.
B) A father is captured and emancipated; he brings his son's wala'.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither A nor B.

Key Takeaway
Jarr flows from higher to lower lineage: a father's capture brings his son's wala', but a son's capture does not bring his father's; for a grandson, the intermediate son's wala' must first be brought.

Muwalah with a Dhimmi: Validity, Inheritance, and the Harbi Distinction
Ruling on permissibility of muwalah between Muslim and dhimmi and inheritance consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muwalah between a Muslim and a dhimmi, derive it via qiyas from wala' al-itiq, explain why inheritance does not occur, and distinguish the case of a harbi.

Core Idea
Muwalah with a dhimmi is valid by qiyas on wala' al-itiq; inheritance is blocked by difference of religion.

This lesson examines the permissibility of a contract of mutual patronage (muwalah) between a Muslim and a non-Muslim who is a dhimmi. The Hanafi school, based on analogy with wala' of emancipation, holds that such a contract is valid. However, inheritance between them is prevented due to difference of religion, a point that might confuse some. We also explore the case of a war captive (harbi), which is treated differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening statement: 'A dhimmi entered into muwalah with a Muslim or a dhimmi; it is permissible, and he is his patron.' The ruling is that the contract is valid (sahih).
The word 'jāz' (permissible) here indicates validity, not mere permissibility.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The author immediately provides the proof: 'because it is permissible for a dhimmi to have wala' of emancipation over a Muslim, and likewise wala' of muwalah.' This is an explicit qiyas (analogy).
The dalil is aqli (reason-based) – qiyas.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalālah
The effective cause ('illa) shared between wala' al-itiq and muwalah is the general concept of walā' (patronage). Since a dhimmi can hold walā' in the first case, he can in the second.
Wala' al-itiq is established by consensus among all schools.
4
Examine the Objection
An objection is raised from al-Muhīt: if a dhimmi enters muwalah with a Muslim and dies, he does not inherit – implying the contract is invalid because inheritance is based on mutual support via religion.
The objection focuses on a consequence, not the contract's validity.
5
Analyse the Reply
The author responds: the contract is valid, but inheritance is blocked by the impediment (māni') of difference of religion. Other rights, such as the obligation to pay blood money ('aql), may still apply if the muwalah agreement includes it.
The key distinction: validity of contract vs. presence of an impediment to a specific effect.
6
Differentiate the Harbi Case
The text extends the discussion to a war captive (harbi). Some hold the contract valid by the same analogy, but others say it is invalid because muwalah implies mutual support, which is prohibited with enemies. The preferred view is that it is invalid.
The prohibition of supporting enemies overrides the analogy for harbi, but not for dhimmi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والي ذمي مسلماً أو ذمياً جاز وهو مولاه لأنه يجوز أن يكون للذمي على المسلم ولاء العتاقة فكذا ولاء الموالاة فإن قلت : قال في المحيط ذمي والى مسلماً فمات لم يرثه لأن الإرث باعتبار التناصر والتناصر في غير القرب إنما هو بالدين، فهذا يفيد أن الموالاة لا تكون بين المسلم والذمي قلنا هي تكون بينهما لكن الإرث إنما يكون حيث لا مانع وحينئذ المانع هنا وهو اختلاف الدين. وإن أسلم على يد ووالاء لم يذكره في الكتاب واختلفوا فيما إذا أعتق الحربي عبده المسلم؛ قيل يصح حربي لأنه لأنه يجوز أن يكون للحربي على المسلم ولاء العتاقة فكذا ولاء الموالاة، وقيل لا يصح عقد المولاة مع الحربي للتناصر وقد نهينا عن ذلك بخلاف الذمي .اهـ.
A dhimmi entered into muwalah with a Muslim or a dhimmi; it is permissible, and he is his patron, because it is permissible for a dhimmi to have wala' of emancipation over a Muslim, and likewise wala' of muwalah. If you say: It is stated in al-Muhit: A dhimmi entered into muwalah with a Muslim and died; he does not inherit from him because inheritance is based on mutual support, and mutual support apart from kinship is only through religion. This indicates that muwalah does not occur between a Muslim and a dhimmi. We reply: It does occur between them, but inheritance only occurs where there is no impediment, and here the impediment is difference of religion. And if a war captive becomes Muslim at someone's hand and enters muwalah, it is not mentioned in the book, and they differed regarding when a war captive emancipates his Muslim slave: some say it is valid for the war captive because it is permissible for a war captive to have wala' of emancipation over a Muslim, and likewise wala' of muwalah. Others say the contract of muwalah with a war captive is not valid because of mutual support, and we have been prohibited from that, unlike the dhimmi. End quote.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage addresses three issues. First, the permissibility of muwalah between a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim) and a Muslim. The ruling is that it is permissible, analogizing to wala' al-itiq where a dhimmi can be a patron of a Muslim slave he frees. The proof is via qiyas: since wala' al-itiq is valid between them, wala' al-muwalah should be too. Second, a potential objection is raised from al-Muhit: if a dhimmi enters muwalah with a Muslim and dies, he does not inherit from him, implying muwalah is not valid because inheritance is based on mutual support (tanasur) which only exists through religion. The reply is that the contract itself is valid, but inheritance is blocked by the impediment of difference of religion. The relationship still gives other rights like 'aql (responsibility for blood money) if the conditions are met. Third, the text discusses the case of a war captive (harbi) who becomes Muslim and enters muwalah, and also the case where the war captive frees his Muslim slave. There is a difference of opinion: some say it is valid because the analogy from wala' al-itiq applies, while others say muwalah with a harbi is invalid because it implies mutual support which is prohibited. The preferred view (as indicated by 'bikhilaf al-dhimmi') is that it is valid for a dhimmi but not for a harbi due to prohibition of supporting enemies.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state who pays jizyah and enjoys protection.
الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of mutual patronage between two parties, historically used to establish inheritance and blood-money responsibility.
الحربي (al-harbi): A non-Muslim not under Islamic protection, effectively an enemy combatant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is muwalah between a Muslim and a dhimmi valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because difference of religion prevents it.
B) Yes, but inheritance does not occur due to difference of religion.
C) Yes, and they inherit from each other.
D) No, because the contract is not allowed with non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Muwalah with a dhimmi is valid by analogy; inheritance is blocked by difference of religion, not by invalidity of the contract.

Buyer's Testimony of Prior Emancipation and Suspended Wala'
Ruling on buyer's testimony and suspended wala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer testifies that the seller had previously emancipated the slave, and explain the status of wala' when the seller denies.

Core Idea
A slave is free upon credible testimony of prior emancipation, but wala' is suspended until the alleged patron confirms.

This passage from al-Mabsut addresses a scenario where a buyer claims the slave he purchased was already freed by the seller. The ruling involves both the immediate freedom of the slave and the suspension of wala' (clientage) pending confirmation. Understanding this clarifies the Hanafi position on testimonies in property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the buyer, seller, and slave in the scenario.
The buyer is the one who purchased the slave.
2
Note the testimony
The buyer testifies that the seller had emancipated the slave prior to the sale.
The testimony is about a past act of emancipation by the seller.
3
Determine the immediate effect
The slave becomes free immediately upon the testimony, assuming it is credible.
Freedom is established even before the wala' is settled.
4
Understand the suspension of wala'
If the seller denies the emancipation, the wala' (right of inheritance and blood money) is suspended, not assigned to anyone yet.
Suspension means the wala' is pending, not transferred to the buyer.
5
Explain the resolution
If the seller later confirms the testimony (or his heirs after his death), then the wala' is established with the seller as the patron.
The wala' reverts to the seller upon confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المبسوط : رجل اشترى من رجل عبداً ثم شهد أن البائع كان أعتقه فهو حر وولاؤه موقوف إذا جحد البائع ذلك فإن صدقه البائع بعد ذلك ظهر أنه المولى وكذا إن صدقه الورثة بعد موته. وفي التتارخانية : رجل من أهل الذمة أعتق عبداً فنقض الذمي العهد ولحق بدار الحرب فأخذ واسترق فصار عبداً لرجل وأراد معتقه أن يوالي رجلاً لم يكن له ذلك لأن مولى العتاقة لا يملك أن يوالي أحداً، فإن أعتق مولاه يوماً من الدهر فإنه يرثه، وإن جنى جناية عقل عن نفسه ولا يعقل عنه مولاه ؛ هكذا ذكر في عامة الروايات وفي بعضها قال : يرثه ويعقل عنه . وإذا أقر الرجل بالولاء لآخر وصدقه يصير مولى له يعقل عنه ويرثه، فإن كان له أولاد كبار فكذبوا الأب فيما أقر وقالوا أبونا مولى لفلان آخر وصدقهم فلان في ذلك فهم مصدقون في حق أنفسهم، وإن قال أعتقني فلان أو فلان وكل منهما يدعي أنه المعتق لا يلزم العبد شيء، وإن أقر بعد ذلك لأحدهما بعينه أو لغيرهما يجوز إقراره على قولهما، وعلى قول الإمام لا يجوز إذا أقر الرجل أنه مولى امرأة أعتقته فقالت المرأة لم أعتقك ليكن أسلمت على يدي وواليتني فهو ،مولاها فإذا أراد التحول عنها إلى غيرها ففي قياس قول الإمام ليس له ذلك، وفي قولهما له ذلك أقر أن فلاناً أعتقه وأنكر فلان وقال ما أعتقتك ولا أعرفك فأقر المقر لأنسان آخر لا يصح إقرار عند الإمام، وعندهما يصي
And in al-Mabsut: A man bought a slave from a man, then testified that the seller had emancipated him; so he is free and his wala' is suspended if the seller denies that. If the seller afterwards affirms him, it becomes clear that he is the patron; likewise if the heirs affirm him after his death. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: A man from the ahl al-dhimmah emancipated a slave, then the dhimmi broke the covenant and joined dar al-harb, so he was captured and enslaved, becoming a slave of a man; his former emancipator wants to enter into muwalah with someone on his behalf; that is not permissible for him, because the patron of emancipation does not have the right to enter into muwalah with anyone. If his patron (the new owner) emancipates him one day, he inherits from him, and if he commits a crime, he pays blood money from himself, and his patron does not pay on his behalf. Thus it is mentioned in most narrations; in some it is said: he inherits from him and pays blood money for him. And when a man acknowledges wala' for another and the other affirms him, he becomes his patron, paying blood money for him and inheriting from him. If he has adult children who deny the father's acknowledgment and say, 'Our father is the client of so-and-so,' and that so-and-so affirms them, then they are believed with regard to themselves. And if a slave says, 'So-and-so emancipated me or so-and-so,' and each claims to be the emancipator, nothing is binding on the slave. If he afterwards acknowledges for one of them specifically or for someone else, his acknowledgment is valid according to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is not valid when a man acknowledges that he is the client of a woman who emancipated him, and the woman says, 'I did not emancipate you; rather you became Muslim at my hand and entered muwalah with me, so you are her client.' Then if he wants to transfer from her to someone else, according to the analogy of Imam Abu Hanifa, he does not have that right; according to the two, he has that right. He acknowledged that so-and-so emancipated him, but so-and-so denied and said, 'I did not emancipate you and I do not know you.' Then the acknowledger acknowledged for another person; according to Imam Abu Hanifa, that acknowledgment is not valid; according to the two, it is valid.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents several independent rulings. First, from al-Mabsut: if a man buys a slave and then testifies that the seller had already freed him, the slave is free but his wala' is suspended until the seller either confirms or denies. If the seller confirms, the wala' returns to him; if the heirs confirm after his death, similarly. Second, from al-Tatarkhaniyyah: a dhimmi emancipates a slave, then the dhimmi apostatizes and joins enemy territory; he is captured and enslaved. The former slave now belongs to a new owner. The former patron (the dhimmi) cannot enter muwalah on behalf of the slave because the patron of emancipation has no right to transfer wala' to another. If the new owner frees the slave, the new owner inherits from him and is not responsible for blood money according to most narrations, though some say he also pays blood money. Third, on acknowledgment: if a man acknowledges that another is his patron (through wala'), and that person confirms, the wala' is established with all rights. However, if the acknowledging man has adult children who deny it and claim that their father is a client of someone else, and that someone else confirms their claim, then the children are believed as far as their own status is concerned (i.e., they are not bound by the father's acknowledgment). Fourth, if a slave says two different people each claim to have freed him, nothing is binding on the slave; but if he later selects one, his acknowledgment is valid according to the two disciples, but not according to Imam Abu Hanifa in certain cases (when there is a counterclaim from a woman who says he became Muslim at her hand and entered muwalah). The final sentence discusses a case where a man acknowledges that a specific person freed him, but that person denies; then he acknowledges for another person. Imam Abu Hanifa says the second acknowledgment is invalid, while the two disciples say it is valid. The underlying principle is that wala' rights are established either by clear evidence or by consistent acknowledgment, but if there is contradiction or denial from those with a competing claim, the ruling is more restrictive according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): The right of inheritance and blood money responsibility between a patron and his freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mabsut, if the seller later confirms the buyer's testimony, whose wala' is established?
A) The buyer's
B) The seller's
C) The state's
D) No one's

Key Takeaway
Testimony of prior emancipation frees the slave immediately, but wala' remains suspended until the alleged patron confirms.

Wala' of a Dhimmi Apostate's Former Slave
Rulings on apostate dhimmi's slave and wala' rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a former dhimmi patron cannot transfer wala' after his slave is re-enslaved, and state the rights of the new owner upon emancipation.

Core Idea
A former dhimmi patron loses all wala' rights when his slave is re-enslaved; only the new owner's emancipation establishes inheritance.

This passage from al-Tatarkhaniyyah discusses a complex scenario: a dhimmi frees a slave, then the dhimmi apostatizes and joins enemy territory, resulting in his own capture and re-enslavement. The former slave now belongs to a new owner. What are the wala' rights of the original patron? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the original patron cannot transfer wala' to another, and the new owner's emancipation establishes inheritance but not blood money responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of events
A dhimmi emancipates a slave → dhimmi apostatizes and joins dar al-harb → dhimmi is captured and enslaved → the former slave becomes owned by a new master.
The dhimmi's apostasy and capture change his legal status completely.
2
Examine the original patron's claim
The original patron (the dhimmi) wants to enter into muwalah (contract of clientage) on behalf of the former slave with someone else.
Muwalah would transfer wala' to another person.
3
State the ruling on the original patron
The original patron cannot do this because 'مولى العتاقة لا يملك أن يوالي أحداً' – the patron of emancipation does not have the right to transfer wala' to another.
Once wala' is established, it is fixed and cannot be assigned.
4
Analyze the new owner's emancipation
If the new owner frees the slave, the new owner inherits from him (يرثه) but according to most narrations, he does not pay blood money (لا يعقل عنه).
Some narrations say he does pay blood money; this is a difference in riwayah.
5
Understand the remedy
The slave's ties to the original patron are severed; only the new owner's emancipation creates a new wala' bond.
The original patron's rights are extinguished by his apostasy and capture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المبسوط : رجل اشترى من رجل عبداً ثم شهد أن البائع كان أعتقه فهو حر وولاؤه موقوف إذا جحد البائع ذلك فإن صدقه البائع بعد ذلك ظهر أنه المولى وكذا إن صدقه الورثة بعد موته. وفي التتارخانية : رجل من أهل الذمة أعتق عبداً فنقض الذمي العهد ولحق بدار الحرب فأخذ واسترق فصار عبداً لرجل وأراد معتقه أن يوالي رجلاً لم يكن له ذلك لأن مولى العتاقة لا يملك أن يوالي أحداً، فإن أعتق مولاه يوماً من الدهر فإنه يرثه، وإن جنى جناية عقل عن نفسه ولا يعقل عنه مولاه ؛ هكذا ذكر في عامة الروايات وفي بعضها قال : يرثه ويعقل عنه . وإذا أقر الرجل بالولاء لآخر وصدقه يصير مولى له يعقل عنه ويرثه، فإن كان له أولاد كبار فكذبوا الأب فيما أقر وقالوا أبونا مولى لفلان آخر وصدقهم فلان في ذلك فهم مصدقون في حق أنفسهم، وإن قال أعتقني فلان أو فلان وكل منهما يدعي أنه المعتق لا يلزم العبد شيء، وإن أقر بعد ذلك لأحدهما بعينه أو لغيرهما يجوز إقراره على قولهما، وعلى قول الإمام لا يجوز إذا أقر الرجل أنه مولى امرأة أعتقته فقالت المرأة لم أعتقك ليكن أسلمت على يدي وواليتني فهو ،مولاها فإذا أراد التحول عنها إلى غيرها ففي قياس قول الإمام ليس له ذلك، وفي قولهما له ذلك أقر أن فلاناً أعتقه وأنكر فلان وقال ما أعتقتك ولا أعرفك فأقر المقر لأنسان آخر لا يصح إقرار عند الإمام، وعندهما يصي
And in al-Mabsut: A man bought a slave from a man, then testified that the seller had emancipated him; so he is free and his wala' is suspended if the seller denies that. If the seller afterwards affirms him, it becomes clear that he is the patron; likewise if the heirs affirm him after his death. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: A man from the ahl al-dhimmah emancipated a slave, then the dhimmi broke the covenant and joined dar al-harb, so he was captured and enslaved, becoming a slave of a man; his former emancipator wants to enter into muwalah with someone on his behalf; that is not permissible for him, because the patron of emancipation does not have the right to enter into muwalah with anyone. If his patron (the new owner) emancipates him one day, he inherits from him, and if he commits a crime, he pays blood money from himself, and his patron does not pay on his behalf. Thus it is mentioned in most narrations; in some it is said: he inherits from him and pays blood money for him. And when a man acknowledges wala' for another and the other affirms him, he becomes his patron, paying blood money for him and inheriting from him. If he has adult children who deny the father's acknowledgment and say, 'Our father is the client of so-and-so,' and that so-and-so affirms them, then they are believed with regard to themselves. And if a slave says, 'So-and-so emancipated me or so-and-so,' and each claims to be the emancipator, nothing is binding on the slave. If he afterwards acknowledges for one of them specifically or for someone else, his acknowledgment is valid according to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is not valid when a man acknowledges that he is the client of a woman who emancipated him, and the woman says, 'I did not emancipate you; rather you became Muslim at my hand and entered muwalah with me, so you are her client.' Then if he wants to transfer from her to someone else, according to the analogy of Imam Abu Hanifa, he does not have that right; according to the two, he has that right. He acknowledged that so-and-so emancipated him, but so-and-so denied and said, 'I did not emancipate you and I do not know you.' Then the acknowledger acknowledged for another person; according to Imam Abu Hanifa, that acknowledgment is not valid; according to the two, it is valid.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents several independent rulings. First, from al-Mabsut: if a man buys a slave and then testifies that the seller had already freed him, the slave is free but his wala' is suspended until the seller either confirms or denies. If the seller confirms, the wala' returns to him; if the heirs confirm after his death, similarly. Second, from al-Tatarkhaniyyah: a dhimmi emancipates a slave, then the dhimmi apostatizes and joins enemy territory; he is captured and enslaved. The former slave now belongs to a new owner. The former patron (the dhimmi) cannot enter muwalah on behalf of the slave because the patron of emancipation has no right to transfer wala' to another. If the new owner frees the slave, the new owner inherits from him and is not responsible for blood money according to most narrations, though some say he also pays blood money. Third, on acknowledgment: if a man acknowledges that another is his patron (through wala'), and that person confirms, the wala' is established with all rights. However, if the acknowledging man has adult children who deny it and claim that their father is a client of someone else, and that someone else confirms their claim, then the children are believed as far as their own status is concerned (i.e., they are not bound by the father's acknowledgment). Fourth, if a slave says two different people each claim to have freed him, nothing is binding on the slave; but if he later selects one, his acknowledgment is valid according to the two disciples, but not according to Imam Abu Hanifa in certain cases (when there is a counterclaim from a woman who says he became Muslim at her hand and entered muwalah). The final sentence discusses a case where a man acknowledges that a specific person freed him, but that person denies; then he acknowledges for another person. Imam Abu Hanifa says the second acknowledgment is invalid, while the two disciples say it is valid. The underlying principle is that wala' rights are established either by clear evidence or by consistent acknowledgment, but if there is contradiction or denial from those with a competing claim, the ruling is more restrictive according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwalah): A contract of clientage between two free persons, establishing wala' rights without emancipation.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim control with which a state of war exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority narrations in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, if the new owner frees the slave, what is his duty regarding the slave's blood money?
A) He must pay blood money for the slave.
B) He is not responsible; the slave pays from himself.
C) He and the slave share the payment.
D) The original patron pays.

Key Takeaway
A patron of emancipation cannot transfer wala' to another; apostasy and re-enslavement nullify his rights.

Acknowledgment of Wala' and Competing Claims
Rulings on acknowledgment of wala' with competing claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of an acknowledgment of wala' when there are competing claims, and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of wala' is binding unless contradicted by certain heirs or by a prior denial; Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the effect of a second acknowledgment after a denial.

This section covers several scenarios involving acknowledgment (iqrar) of wala'. A person may acknowledge that another is his patron, but this can be contested by the acknowledged's children, by the slave himself, or by a third party claiming to be the true emancipator. The passage also presents a key ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the validity of a second acknowledgment after the first is denied. Understanding these rules helps clarify the Hanafi approach to establishing wala' through unilateral statements.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic acknowledgment rule
If a man acknowledges that another is his patron (through wala'), and that person confirms, the wala' is established with all rights (inheritance, blood money).
Mutual acknowledgment is sufficient to create wala' even without evidence of emancipation.
2
Examine the children's denial
If the acknowledging man has adult children who deny his statement and claim their father is a client of someone else, and that someone else confirms their claim, the children are believed with regard to themselves.
They are not bound by their father's acknowledgment; they can claim a different patron for themselves.
3
Analyze the slave's conflicting statements
If a slave says two different people each claim to have freed him, nothing is binding on the slave. If he later acknowledges one specifically, his acknowledgment is valid according to the two disciples.
Imam Abu Hanifa restricts this when there is a counterclaim from a woman who says the slave became Muslim at her hand and entered muwalah.
4
Evaluate the woman's claim
A man says he is the client of a woman who freed him; she denies emancipation but says he became Muslim at her hand and entered muwalah. The man wants to transfer to another. According to Abu Hanifa, he cannot; according to the two, he can.
The dispute centers on whether muwalah can be unilaterally terminated.
5
Resolve the final case: second acknowledgment after denial
A man says 'X freed me'; X denies. Then the man says 'Y freed me'. Imam Abu Hanifa says the second acknowledgment is invalid; the two disciples say it is valid and creates wala' with Y.
This is the crux of the ikhtilaf: can a person change his claim after being denied?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in al-Mabsut: A man bought a slave from a man, then testified that the seller had emancipated him; so he is free and his wala' is suspended if the seller denies that. Key Terms
الإقرار بالولاء (al-iqrar bi al-wala'): A unilateral acknowledgment that another person is one's patron in wala' (either emancipation or muwalah).

Checkpoint

A) Valid, because the man can clarify the truth.
B) Invalid, because the first denial makes the second unreliable.
C) Valid, but only if Amr confirms.
D) Invalid, regardless of Amr's confirmation.

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment of wala' is generally binding, but a second acknowledgment after a denial is contested: Abu Hanifa invalidates it, the two disciples validate it.

Transfer of Muwalah and Prohibition for Freed Persons
Rulings on transfer of muwalah and prohibition for freed persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid transfer of a muwalah client and explain why a freed person by emancipation cannot enter muwalah.

Core Idea
Transfer of muwalah is allowed before the patron bears financial responsibility; after that, it becomes binding like a gift with compensation.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine the rules governing the transfer of a client (al-muwalah) from one patron to another. The key distinction is whether the original patron has already borne financial responsibility for the client. We also explore why a person who has been freed by emancipation cannot engage in muwalah, and how the rulings differ for female clients and their children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The contract of muwalah is not binding (ghayr lazim). Each party can rescind it with the knowledge of the other, unless the other is absent. Transfer to another patron is permissible before the original patron has borne any financial responsibility (al-'aql).
The contract remains non-binding until one party actually performs an obligation like paying blood money.
2
Locate the condition for transfer
The text explicitly states: 'ما لم يعقل عنه' (as long as he has not borne responsibility for him). Once the patron has paid blood money or provided financial support, the contract becomes strengthened (ta'akkada) and cannot be unilaterally transferred. It becomes like a gift with compensation (hibah bi al-'iwad).
This principle also applies to the child after bearing responsibility for the father.
3
Understand the automatic rescission (faskh hukmi)
If the client (the lower party) contracts a new muwalah with another patron without the presence or knowledge of the first patron, the new contract is valid and the first is automatically rescinded. This is because muwalah is like lineage (ka al-nasab) and cannot attach to two patrons simultaneously.
Analogous to agency, mudarabah, and partnership: a subsequent conflicting contract rescinds the earlier one without explicit cancellation.
4
Distinguish wala' al-itiq from wala' al-muwalah
A person who has been freed by emancipation (wala' al-itiq) cannot enter into muwalah because the wala' of emancipation is permanent (lazim) and takes precedence over muwalah in inheritance. The proof: if a person dies with both a patron of emancipation and a patron of muwalah, the emancipator inherits exclusively.
This prohibition applies to the freed person himself; he cannot have client relationships through muwalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منه إلى غيره بمحضر من الآخر ما لم يعقل عنه وليس للمعتق أن يوالي أحداً ولو والت امرأة فولدت تبعها فيه . وفي المحيط : ولو ادعى رجل ولاء الموالاة وأقام البينة وادعى آخر مثل ذلك وأقام البينة فالمتأخر أولى لأنه يحتمل الفسخ بخلاف ولاء العتاقة .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وهو آخر ذوي الأرحام إذا لم يكن له وارث غير ذوي الأرحام فإرثه له) قال رحمه الله : (وله أن يتحول منه إلى غيره بمحضر من الآخر ما لم يعقل عنه لأن العقد غير لازم كالوصية والوكالة ولكل واحد منهما أن يفسخه بعلم الآخر، فإن كان الآخر غائباً لا يملك فسخه وإن كان غير لازم لأن العقد تم لهما كما في الشركة والمضاربة والوكالة ولا يعرى عن ضرر لأنه ربما يموت الأسفل فيأخذ الأعلى ميراثه فيكون مضموناً عليه، أو يعقل الأسفل عبيداً على حسبان أن عقل عبيده على المولى الأعلى فيجب عليه وحده فيتضرر بذلك فلا يصح الفسخ إلا بمحضر من الآخر بخلاف ما إذا عقد الأسفل الموالاة مع غيره بغير محضر من حتى يصح وينفسخ العقد الأول لأنه فسخ حكمي فلا يشترط فيه العلم كما في الوكالة والمضاربة والشركة لأن الموالاة كالنسب إذا ثبت من شخص كونه مع غيره فينفسخ ضرورة، والمرأة في هذا كالرجل. وقوله ما لم يعقل عنه لأنه إذا عقل عنه ليس له أن يتحول إلى غيره لتأكده بتعلق حق الغير به لحصول المعقود به ولاتصال العصوبة ولأن ولاية التحول قبل أن يعقل عنه باعتبار أنه عقد تبرع، فإذا عقل عنه صار كالعوض في الهبة، وكذا لا يتحول ولده بعد ما تحمل الجناية عن أبيه، وكذا إن عقل عن ولده لم يكن لكل واحد منهما أن يتحول إلى غيره لأنهما كشخص واحد في حكم الولاء. قال رحمه الله : (وليس للمعتق أن يوالي (أحداً لأن ولاء العتاقة لازم لا يحتمل النقض بعد ثبوته فلا ينفسخ ولا ينعقد معه لأنه لا ينقل لأن الإرث بولاء العتاقة مقدم على الإرث بولاء الموالاة، ألا ترى أن شخصاً لو مات وترك مولى عتقه ومولى موالاته كان المال للمعتق. قال في المبسوط : ولو مات الأعلى ثم مات الأسفل فإنما يرثه المذكور من أولاد الأعلى دون الإناث على نحو ما بينا في ولاء العتاقة. قال رحمه الله : (ولو والت امرأة فولدت تبعها فيه) يعني ولدت ولداً لا يعرف له أب وكذا لو أقرت أنها مولاة فلان ومعها ولد صغير لا يعرف له أب صح إقرارها على نفسها ويتبعها ولدها فيه، وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : يتبعها ولدها فيه في الصورتين وقد تقدم بيان ذلك . (فروع) عبد الحربي خرج مستأمناً في تجارة لمولاه
From him to someone else in the presence of the other as long as he has not borne responsibility for him. And it is not permissible for the freed person (by emancipation) to enter into muwalah with anyone. And if a woman enters muwalah and gives birth, the child follows her in it. And in al-Muhit: If a man claims wala' of muwalah and provides evidence, and another claims similarly and provides evidence, the later one is more deserving because it can be rescinded, unlike wala' of emancipation. End quote. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (It is the last of the dhawi al-arham if he has no heir other than dhawi al-arham, then inheritance is for him). He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And he has the right to transfer from it to someone else in the presence of the other as long as he has not borne responsibility for him), because the contract is not binding like a will and power of attorney, and each one of them has the right to rescind it with the knowledge of the other. If the other is absent, he does not have the right to rescind it even though it is not binding, because the contract is valid for both, as in a partnership, mudarabah, and agency, and it is not free from harm because perhaps the lower one dies and the upper one takes his inheritance and becomes liable for it, or the lower one bears responsibility for slaves thinking that the burden of his slaves is on the upper patron, so he alone becomes obligated and thereby harmed. Therefore rescission is not valid except in the presence of the other, contrary to when the lower one contracts muwalah with someone else without the presence of the first, then it is valid and the first contract is rescinded because it is a legal rescission, so knowledge is not required as in agency, mudarabah, and partnership, because muwalah is like lineage: when one person is established with another, it is necessarily rescinded. And the woman is like the man in this. His saying 'as long as he has not borne responsibility for him' because if he has borne responsibility for him, he does not have the right to transfer to another, due to its being strengthened by the attachment of the right of another to him, due to the occurrence of the contracted purpose and the connection of patronage, and because the authority to transfer before bearing responsibility is based on its being a gratuitous contract. When he bears responsibility, it becomes like a gift with compensation. Likewise, his child does not transfer after the burden of the crime for his father. Likewise, if he bears responsibility for his child, neither of them has the right to transfer to another because they are like one person in the ruling of wala'. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is not permissible for the freed person (by emancipation) to enter into muwalah with anyone), because wala' of emancipation is binding and does not admit of rescission after its establishment, so it is not rescinded and it is not contracted with anyone because it is not transferable. Indeed, inheritance through wala' of emancipation takes precedence over inheritance through wala' of muwalah. Do you not see that if a person dies leaving a patron of emancipation and a patron of muwalah, the property goes to the emancipator. It is said in al-Mabsut: And if the upper one dies and then the lower one dies, the mentioned one from the children of the upper one inherits from him, but not the females, as we explained in wala' of emancipation. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if a woman enters muwalah and gives birth, the child follows her in it), meaning she gives birth to a child whose father is not known. Likewise, if she acknowledges that she is the client of so-and-so and she has a small child whose father is not known, her acknowledgment is valid regarding herself and her child follows her in it. This according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the child follows her in both cases, and this has been explained before. (Branches) A slave of a war captive went out as a musta'min for trade for his patron.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a comprehensive treatment of the transferability of muwalah and the prohibition for freed persons. First, the text establishes that a patron may transfer his client to another patron as long as he has not borne any financial responsibility (e.g., paid blood money) on his behalf. The contract of muwalah is not binding; it can be rescinded by either party with the knowledge of the other. However, if one party is absent, the contract cannot be unilaterally rescinded because of potential harm. But if the client himself contracts a new muwalah with another patron without informing the first, the new contract is valid and the first is automatically rescinded by judicial rescission (faskh hukmi), similar to agency or partnership. This is because muwalah is like lineage; it cannot exist with two patrons simultaneously. The text then explains the condition 'as long as he has not borne responsibility': once the patron has actually paid blood money or provided financial support, the contract becomes stronger and cannot be unilaterally rescinded; it becomes like a gift with exchange. This also applies to the child after bearing responsibility. Second, the text clarifies that a person who is freed by emancipation (wala' al-itiq) cannot enter into muwalah with anyone because wala' al-itiq is permanent and cannot be transferred or superseded. The proof is that inheritance from wala' al-itiq takes precedence over muwalah; if someone dies with both types of patrons, the emancipator gets the entire inheritance. Third, the text discusses the female client: if a woman enters muwalah and then gives birth to a child with no known father, the child follows her in muwalah (i.e., belongs to her patron). If she acknowledges that she is a client of someone and has a child with unknown father, her acknowledgment is valid for herself and the child follows her, according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two disciples say the child follows her in both cases. Finally, a branch ruling: a slave of a war captive who comes as a musta'min (safe-conduct holder) for trade belongs to his patron's wala'.

Key Terms
العقل (al-'aql): Bearing financial responsibility for blood money (diyah) or compensation on behalf of the client.
الفسخ الحكمي (al-faskh al-hukmi): Automatic rescission of a contract due to a conflicting subsequent contract, without requiring explicit cancellation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a patron transfer his client to another patron in muwalah?
A) At any time, even if he has paid blood money.
B) Only with the client's permission.
C) As long as he has not borne any financial responsibility for the client.
D) Only if the client agrees to the transfer.

Key Takeaway
Transfer of muwalah is permissible only before the patron bears financial responsibility; after that, it becomes binding, and freed persons cannot engage in muwalah.

Definition and Legal Proofs of Coercion (Ikrah)
Opening of the chapter on coercion with definition and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and juristic definition of ikrah, identify its pillar, and cite the Quranic and Hadith evidence for its legal effect.

Core Idea
Coercion transfers the legal effect of an action from the coerced to the coercer, as shown by the Quran and the hadith of Safwan.

This lesson introduces the chapter on ikrah (coercion) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. We will examine why this chapter is placed after wala' al-mawalat, and then explore the linguistic and technical definitions of ikrah, its essential pillar (the threatening word), and the primary textual proofs from the Quran and Sunnah. Understanding this foundational definition is crucial for applying the many rulings on coerced actions in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transition
Notice why the author placed the chapter on ikrah after wala' al-mawalat: both involve a change from prohibition to permissibility. This highlights the legal effect of coercion.
The parallel is that the condition of the person changes — in wala' from haram to halal, and here from haram to halal for the coerced.
2
Distinguish linguistic from juristic definition
The linguistic definition is given: forcing a person to something he dislikes. The juristic definition is deferred but hinted at: it is the word that conveys coercion (the pillar) and its conditions.
The juristic definition will be completed later in the text.
3
Extract the pillar (rukn)
The pillar of ikrah is identified as 'the word that conveys it' — i.e., the threatening utterance that forces the coerced to act.
This is a verbal threat; the coercer must utter a word that instills fear and compels action.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse 'except one who is forced' (16:106) shows that a coerced person is excused from the sin of apostasy, implying that coercion removes culpability.
This establishes the principle that the coerced is not blameworthy.
5
Analyze the Hadith evidence
The hadith of Safwan al-Ta'i: The Prophet upheld the coerced divorce but did not punish Safwan, showing that the divorce is valid but the coerced is not liable, and the effect is attributed to the coercer.
This hadith is key for understanding how liability shifts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه

أورد الإكراه عقيب ولاء الموالاة لأن في كل منهما تغير حال المخاطب من الحزمة إلى الحل، إن ولاء الموالاة يغير حال المخاطب الذي هو المولى الأعلى من حرمة تناول مال المولى الأسفل بعد موته إلى حله، بالإرث فكذا الإكراه يغير حال المخاطب الذي هو المكره من حرمة المباشرة إلى حلها؛ كذا في عامة المواضع. والكلام فيه في مواضع : الأول في معناه لغة، والثاني عند الفقهاء، والثالث في ركنه والرابع في دليله والخامس في شرطه، والسادس في حكمه. فهو في اللغة عبارة عن حمل إنسان على شيء يكره يقال أكرهت فلاناً إكراها أي حملته على أمر يكرهه . وهو عند الفقهاء ما سيأتي. وركنه اللفظ الذي يفيده ودليله من الكتاب قوله تعالى إلا من أكره ﴾ [النحل : ١٠٦] الآية. ومن السنة ما ورد أن صفوان الطائي كان نائماً مع امرأته وأخذت المرأة سكيناً وجلست على صدره وقالت لأذبحنك أو تطلقني فناشدها بالله فأبت فطلقها ثلاثاً فبلغ ذلك إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : لا إقالة في الطلاق وشرطه سيأتي في الكتاب. وحكمه إذا حصل به إتلاف أن ينتقل إلى المكره فيما يصلح أن يكون آلة للمكره ويجعل كأنه فعله بنفسه كما سيجيء.
The Book of Coercion. Ikrah is placed after the chapter on wala' al-mawalat because in each there is a change in the condition of the addressed person from prohibition to permissibility. Indeed, wala' al-mawalat changes the condition of the addressed, who is the higher patron, from the prohibition of taking the property of the lower patron after his death to its permissibility through inheritance. Similarly, ikrah changes the condition of the addressed, who is the coerced person, from the prohibition of direct action to its permissibility. This is the case in most texts. Discussion about it occurs in six places: first, its linguistic meaning; second, its meaning according to jurists; third, its pillar; fourth, its evidence; fifth, its condition; sixth, its ruling. Linguistically, it is the act of forcing a person to something he dislikes. It is said, 'I coerced so-and-so,' meaning I forced him to a matter he dislikes. According to jurists, it is what will come. Its pillar is the word that conveys it. Its evidence from the Book is the Almighty's saying, 'Except one who is forced' (Quran 16:106) - the verse. From the Sunnah, what is reported that Safwan al-Ta'i was sleeping with his wife, and the woman took a knife and sat on his chest and said, 'I will slaughter you or you divorce me.' He adjured her by Allah, but she refused, so he divorced her three times. That reached the Prophet (peace be upon him), who said: 'There is no cancellation in divorce.' Its condition will come in the book. Its ruling is that if it results in destruction, it is transferred to the coercer in matters suitable for the coerced person to be a tool, and it is made as if he did it himself, as will come.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on ikrah (coercion) in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It first explains why the chapter follows wala' al-mawalat: both involve a change from prohibition to permissibility. The author then lists six topics to be discussed: linguistic meaning, technical meaning, pillar, evidence, condition, and ruling. The linguistic definition is given: 'forcing a person to something he dislikes.' The technical definition is deferred. The pillar (rukn) is identified as the word that conveys coercion. Two proofs are provided: a Quranic verse (16:106) that indicates the excuse for the coerced, and a hadith about Safwan who was forced to divorce, and the Prophet upheld the divorce, showing that coercion does not invalidate the action but the coerced is excused. The ruling is briefly stated: if coercion leads to destruction (e.g., of property), the responsibility is shifted to the coercer, as if he did it himself.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Act of forcing a person to do something he dislikes, linguistically; juristically, it is defined later in the text as an act that removes consent or corrupts choice without removing legal capacity.
اللفظ (al-lafdh): The word that conveys coercion; the pillar (rukn) of ikrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the pillar (rukn) of ikrah according to the passage?
A) The threat of death
B) The word that conveys coercion
C) The acceptance of the coerced
D) The physical presence of the coercer

Key Takeaway
Coercion is defined linguistically as forcing a person to do something disliked; its legal effect is to transfer liability to the coercer while the coerced action remains valid.
